I believe that Grabby Aliens and the Dark Forest options can be discarded through thought experiment.  Civilization reaching space faring stage requires a ton of co-operation of the individuals making up that species.  I think it's a reasonably safe assumption to look at our cultural past and see how we keep moving away from colonial powers and empires that seek to dominate us and project that same experience on to most other species.  Science and Technology are about advancement through sharing ideas: why would we think aliens would be as successful with less co-operation.  One of the primary arguments for Grabby Aliens has been about resource utilization, but the more we come to know about exoplanets the more it seems that if you have access to FTL the less you'd need to worry about utilizing planets that are already occupied.  Before we go anywhere we'd be likely to send a drone: why do we think they wouldn't?  If they know we're here, why not go to a different solar system to stock up?  For that matter, if they know we're here why not say "hi"?  Once you think it through, Grabby Aliens seems borderline absurd.  And so, for anyone who has done this reasoning the Dark Forest theory also falls apart.

A quickie for the ones who want a fast answer (I havent watched the video, this is just an answer from my previous knowledge they might mention something completely different so do watch the video)  - yes there are, but they are too unstable to exist for a period of time longer than a fraction of a second for some and a couple hiurs for others.  A bit more detail - atoms of elements have a number of neutrons and protons in their core, they defy what element the element actually is. The number of protons defies the element while the number of neutrons defies which izotope of the element it is. An izotope is like a different version of the same element. For example Ozone is actually and izotope of oxygen and not a unique element.   So the amount of protons and neutrons matters for the stability of the element, there exist numbers called "magic numbers" which indicate how stable an atom is, if it has one of these magic numbers as the amount of protons or neutrons, then the atom is more stable, thats also the reason why most higher elements de-grade to Lead, as Lead is a magic number and one of its izotopes is also doublely-magic having a magic number of neutrons and protons.   This stability of elements can be put in a chart called "the valley of stability" there you can see a clear pstter of ehich elements are stable and which are not, but the pattern ends at a point, after which there are no known stable elements that can be created. For even further info I'd reccomend you to watch the video about the man who tried to steal an element by a creator whose name I forgot, its the one that has Nivmonium on the thumbnail.

Given that there are no stable elements beyond lead, and atoms seem to only get more unstable the more massive they get, we're highly unlikely to find any more new elements that still exist in nature. All the "new" elements we've "discovered" in recent decades were the result of smashing atoms together in particle accelerators to see if they'd combine, creating single atoms of super-heavy elements which then decayed again in a fraction of a second.

Can they gather together (like normal matter does) to form macro-objects? Such as asteroids, planets - even sun-sized or larger objects? It wouldn't be like an impact with another normal matter object (or would it?), but it would still have a gravitational field that would interact with the gravitational field of the normal matter object. I can't help but think of a conversation I had some years ago with my friend Somaditya Sen, a world-renowned physicist whom I only knew because our kids went to school together. In those days we lived very close together and had many, many far-ranging conversations about science and the nature of reality as we naked apes conceived of it.   One day, I told Somaditya about a very popular book making the rounds at the time called The End of Science, wherein the author postulated that we silly humans, who had only recently discovered electricity, had pretty much figured out the universe, with perhaps less than 1% of what there was to know was left to be mapped out, a mere mopping up operation. My jovial, genius friend erupted in such a fit of uncontrollable laughter that I feared for his health.   When he finally caught his breath again, and had wiped the tears of laughter from his face, he responded that, if anything, those percentages were actually the inverse. That we had only maybe understood 1% of what there was to know. Put another way, if we lofty beings had only harnessed steam power a few centuries ago, what might a civilization that had evolved a million or even a billion years ago have learned about the one universe we presume to understand?

Adamantium is not an element in the Marvel universe. Adamantium is a nigh-unbreakable steel alloy, the exact composition of which is a highly guarded secrete by the US government. It does not occur in nature.   Vibranium, on the other hand, is an alien metal which does occur in nature in the Marvel universe. Wakanda has so much vibranium because it sits on the site of an asteroid crash.

5:33 saying that elements have only unstable isotopes is a bit false information. There is theoretically (almost) unlimited amount of possible isotopes, and we actually do not know which may be stable in reality. If we knew, we would not doubt about "island of stability" existing or not. Correct way to say it would be: we have discovered and/or produced only unstable isotopes.

Each larger element is simply the addition of a proton in the nucleus. Isotopes of that element are created by varying the neutrons in the nucleus, and ions are created by varying the number of electrons in the orbitals. Generally, as atoms get larger, they get more unstable (though this is not strictly always the case), so it becomes more and more difficult to find larger elements ocuring in nature. However larger elements can always be theoretically artificially created by combining smaller elements together. Therefore there are necessarily larger elements than what have been discovered.

Isotope lives matter! The standard table of elements doesn't mention isotopes. Isotopes have different properties from the elements on the table. Anyone who tries to assemble nuclear fuel knows this. Maybe there are new isotopes of existing elements that have special properties. For example, Deuterium and Tritium are isotopes of Hydrogen, but maybe we just haven't discovered Quadritium, Quintitium, Sexitium, etc. (i.e., 4, 5 and 6 neutrons.) What we weren't aware of was just how simple it was. When we had all the knowledge to allow us to convert it from one to another. Just as we recognize that in food chain, and in the animal kingdom, we have links, and once we break that chain or a link goes missing we have to live with the repercussions that may well have on everything else.  Everything is inextricably connected, and it doesn't matter whether we knew it or not, the truth of the matter is that it is. This will be difficult for humans to process, as we are restricted in our ability to think outside of the box so to speak.   In the last 6 to 7 decades we have been adding new elements which we refer to as "Exotic" because they are not found here on our planet but we have used the oldest system known to man and that is we have bartered for them. It is not important what the trade off was, it's the fact that we have, and more than on one occasion.   Lets be clear about trade off's as this is more so about having a "Supplier" in the same way as a drug dealer maybe referred to as a supplier. So we have the so called galactic version who can get their hands on anything. In the past 70 years it's been a bit of a sweet shop moment whereby what has been shown to us, is both mind blowing and above top secret, on levels one cannot even begin to understand.  So the paradox we now face is how do you introduce what clearly isn't known to us on planet earth. One route is claim it was just a passenger on a passing meteorite, and we've just managed to extract it! Like anyone is going to believe that one. This is where you have to be slightly more creative, and say well we dredged it from the bottom of the ocean, which allows it to be remotely plausible. Hmm. not sure with that one either.   Or we go for the unconvincing well we've just discovered it Antarctica and then nobody can contest it. The other problem is releasing this new element into mainstream and before you know it we cannot get enough of it for it to be commercialized. The idea that a second evolution of life would be "eaten" by the prior one is dubious. There is no reason to expect the two disparate evolutions to be at all compatible, and may even be toxic enough to prevent many of the same systems that would make them interact, like parasitism or commensalism etc. If one had a different method of forming a genetic database, by using different amino acids or even different sugars, even just one substitution from our "normal" notion of DNA would make these differing lifeforms unable to interact biologically, no good as food, no good as potential gene swapping partner. Although, it would be interesting to ponder just how far disparate genetic systems might be able to hybridize, after all, our code for life is not that complicated , A-T and C-G is simply a binary code that gives rise to higher forms of complexity going up the structure types, but given this, imagine a form of DNA that utilizes say TEN pairs of complimentary base pairs ! The possibility then exists for the coding of proteins just as complex or more than ours but with the use of far fewer base pairs, a life form as complex as us but with a genetic instruction set only a tenth or less in size...it may be that any newly discovered genetic system on a far away planet that uses more than our simple binary system would have wildly diverse and capable biological abilities, and look upon us as extremely simple creatures...but then I could be wrong lol.

Unobtainium in the Avatar universe annoys me because it's a commonly used technical term for speculative design. It is simply a material with properties that do not fit any known material. The designer can set what properties are necessary, and then material scientists can work to create or discover a material that fits that description. Once you actually make it you give it a real name, and it is no longer unobtainium. As they have discovered the real thing in that universe they would then name it, and no longer call it unobtainium.

Technically, it is possible to have a really heavy element beyond the current periodic table. The problem is they tend to be extremely unstable.  Our current methods of getting them as well, are... bombarding them with nuclear reactions. Which end up creating lesser variants until they get a relatively stable element that doesn't immediately decay. Element 114, for example has a half life of 1.9 seconds, and there's an unconfirmed isotope of it, 290 with a half life of 19 seconds. The element is theorized to be a super conductor. Which if possible to have something with a half-life of more than 10s, we could create a fast propulsion system out of it.  The major problem is the instability, and radioactivity of 92+
When I was in high school the periodic table went to 103, although several isotopes of 104  were synthesized in '69, '70 and '74 with 105 was '68 and '70. We've apparently been stuck at 118 for 20 years now.
Regarding medical imaging: Tc is used in nuclear/scint/gamma cameras/SPECT. This video shows CT images (made from X-ray), which is a completely different technology.  The two groups of images look completely different. The X-ray CT images show anatomy at high resolution. The Tc images show function, such as metabolism, activity, spread of fluids etc.

Don't pontificate too hard about the anthropic principle.  Some of it is chance and by definition unsolvable.  As an example,  you buy 8 lotto tickets but you can't see the numbers.  If one wins the prize, it's reasonable to assume 1 in 8 tickets win.  We all know this is false,  but if you never encounter another lotto, nor get access to the selection process,  how are you going to know? 1 in 8 will remain the assumption... and that's ok. Stop worrying and get some sleep :)

Sorry to correct you, but the atomic number is based on the amount of electrons - not protons, the number of protons change from element to element, but when the atomic number goes up, the amount of electrons change with it (for example, if a electron found it's way into a hydrogen atom, it would become a helium atom, with no neutrons and 1 proton)

16:35 Interesting solution for the Fermi Paradox there. We are (one of) the first, since assuming a wrong starting point (we are a "standard" civilisation) is a much easier solution than postulating other hypothetical solutions which rely on some random extinction event that kills EVERY civilisation (if some where left over we would see them). etc. pp.  So you could even argue: The fact that there are no communicating space faring civilisations proves that we are not randomly selected but instead one of the first civilisations. Quick comment: the `strong force' and the `strong nuclear force' are not the same. Essentially, the latter is a residual force from the former, usually just called the `nuclear force'. The strong force shows up in the standard model as one of the fundamental interactions, but the nuclear force doesn't.

What are "preciseley balanced" elements in our list of known elements - the top notch peaks of this "island" of stability : gold, platinum, titanium. These won't decay within foreseeable "infinity" of time. Stable nucleuses and stable electron shells neither being e-negative nor e-positive. Just passive. Also they are super-unwilling to react at room temperature or simply unreactive except for if put under massive thermal stress (loosens electrons because of strong thermal movement - amplitude resp. -exciting) or being dissolved by "combined/reciped" super-aggressive acids to force some electrons free for becoming ions. Interestingly these balanced elements have proven to have katalytic properties to make reactions possible which otherwise are difficult to facilitate.

I wouldn't say Americium is critical for smokedetectors. Ionic smoke detectors have have been replaced by optical detectors using lasers and mirrors. The sensitivity for these optical detectors can be programmed (not for all types) and they can also include a thermal detector, which allows you to even more specifically program when the detector comes into alarm (combining info from both the optical as well as thermal inputs to know whether there is a real fire or whether it's just dust or humidity). Old ionic detectors containing americium can still be found in old installations, but there aren't a lot of them left and those still present in buildings are being replaced by simple optical or thermal detectors or detectors that contain both optical and thermal instruments; newer ones containing 2 optical chambers and a temp sensor, as well as a tiny amount of memory on a chip to save the data that occured 10 seconds before and after the detector came in alarm.

Great video. Loved it.   There are a few tiny errors in the brief section about Tc in medical imaging. Tc-99m with its 140 keV photon is the "work-horse" of nuclear radiology, and we use a gamma camera to collect those photons. There is a kernel of truth in that it plays an important role in medical imaging. However, it's not a "contrast agent" as radiologists typically use the term, and none of the images shown were of a nuclear medicine study using Tc-99m. The contrast agents used in the images you showed were gadolinium-based (MRI) and iodine-based (CT). These elements do have useful physical properties, but radioactivity isn't among them. Apologies if this has already been pointed out.

Originally we were supposed to get an island of stability around element 114, but they just keep getting more unstable as we make heavier elements.  The heavier elements seem to be caught in a squeeze in which those that do not have a high enough neutron to proton ratio alpha decay very rapidly, whereas those that have a higher neutron to proton ratio spontaneously fission (you see this first with fermium, of which isotope 257 is the most stable isotope, alpha decaying with a half-life of 100.5 days, and then isotope 258 spontaneously fissions in less than a millisecond (and note that the even-even rule of stability doesn't work here and for some other transuranium elements), and isotopes 259 and 260 also spontaneously fission very quickly.  After fermium, you get a slight break with mendelevium, for which you can get the highest few isotopes to have enough neutrons that they can actually beta decay, but then starting with nobelium anything that doesn't alpha decay spontaneously fissions, and it gets faster until by the time you get to the upper end of what we have been able to make, you can't do anything with what you have made other than confirm detection.  Current technology (ramming calcium 48 into a heavy element) should in principle get us to Element 121 -- in principle, it should be possible to make macroscopic quantities of einsteinium (actually, we already did that at least once, with einsteinium 253, half life 20.47 days, and that isn't even the most stable isotope), Fermium 257 (half-life 100.5 days), and mendelevium 260 (half life 27.8 days); adding 20 protons gets you Elements 119, 120, and 121, respectively.  After that, you would have to use a heavier projectile (they're already trying titanium 50, with no success so far), but then you won't be able to get as high a neutron to proton ratio, so alpha decay instability will be even worse, and then you really won't be able to detect what you have made, let alone do anything with it.

It's worth considering that some of the elements we call "radioactive" due in large part to where they are on the Periodic Table, ie. how heavy, are essentially extremely stable, almost not radioactive -- U-238 having a half-life of 4 and a half billion years. Correction here: elements 119+ have not been discovered yet because their predicted production rates are far lower than the elements preceding them. Element 119 should have a lifetime very close to element 118, and elements 120-121 should have half lives above 1 microsecond, long enough to observe in current experimental setups.

Good IQ question: [2, 8, 20, 28, 50, 82, 126] - what's next? The best approximation people have got is the n > 3 part of A162626 sequence, saying "184". However, this sequence part, when n > 3: n*(n**2+5)/3, only coincides with [28, 50, 82, 126], but not with [2, 8, 20]. It would be fun to find a mathematical sequence that coincides with [2, 8, 20, 28, 50, 82, 126] without resorting to a separate formula for each part.

The idea that the first lifeform on the planet was our ancestors because if we were the second, then the first would have eaten us is flawed.  Why?  It is VERY possible for there to be no end of prior lifeforms, and that all of those died out - going extinct before it got large enough to even see in the fossil record.  Their detritus would have littered the planet with the 'trash' of evolution, and over time, altered the chemical makeup of the planet to allow our ancestors to begin.  By that measure, the normal track for evolution is actually extinction.  Consider the oxygen extinction event we know DID happen, and also, the extinction event that happened in the Cretaceous, it may well be we've plenty of evidence that extinction is the 'normal' track for evolution.  The ones that survive are the oddities....

Nice presentation. Like add a point regarding contrast agents in medical imaging at 14:30. CT brain images were shown. Technetium is not used for computerized Tomography (CT) or MRI. CT uses injected non-ionic iodine (not radioactive) where MRI uses Gadolinium. Technetium 99m is used in Nuclear Medicine not as contrast agents but to actively visualize the organ or tissue it been tagged to. Different biologically- active substances are combined to TC99m to image the skeleton, liver, kidney or even detect infection. TC99M emits gamma rays and low energy electrons. After injection and appropriate time interval, a gamma camera using sodium iodide crystals images the body part. It has a 6 hour half-life.

Short answer: No.  With the discovery of Og (element 118) the periods are full. The next element (#119) would require an additional new shell outside of the two 32-electron shells. It would be a 64-electron shell. But please note that every element above 92 is not naturally occurring and is highly unstable. Most transuranic elements  -- Neptunium and Plutonium being significant exceptions -- exist in a collider for picoseconds or fractions thereof before decaying.   That is because the nuclei are very nearly outside -- or are partially outside -- the limits of the Strong Nuclear Force. The first barbell-shaped pulse throws a significant amount of the nucleus outside the limits of the SNF, resulting in some form of decay.   If there existed a new shell -- which BTW, would make the Periodic table double in width overnight -- the resulting nucleus would be too unstable for us to observe it. It probably couldn't exist at all, because not even a nuclear collider would fit that entire nucleus inside the SNF boundaries.    So, no, there cannot, in any practical terms, exist any element above 118.

pfft  Nice little insertion of fermi paradox.   The solution is simple.  The universe is large is both size, and time.  There could be literally trillions of civilisations in a single galaxy.  But due to the distance seperating them, and the time scales that they appear; it would be uncommon for two to actually meet.    The best best is archeology.. but even then youd have to be doing your digs thousands of metres underground; and they would need to have created something durable and large enough to survive long enough at that depth /we/ find the remains.  Even in our OWN history how many civilisations have formed risen and fallen in the last 5000 years alone?  Of those how many have almost no record they existed at all other than a few fluke finds deep in cave systems?  They once found an "alien coin"  (ultimately the concensus is it was a hoax) but the coin was supposedly found in a deep core sample from a drilling rig.   This is the type of evidence we are most likely to find.  So had it not been a hoax: 1: it was a culture that created/collected things 2: It was a culture with a concept of economics and created currency 3: It was a culture that developed a written form of expression 4: It was made out of a material that could last a million years at a deep depth unchanged.  All 4 of these would have to apply for us to even find a coin from a lost civilisation that existed thousands or millions of years ago.

As a chemist, I HAD to watch this video, to see if they screwed it up. They didn't!! It was very well done! In regards to this topic, what I like to think about is not just the properties of this future-element, but the properties that we might discover when we alloy this future-element with other metals. Or when we react this future-element with other organic/ inorganic compounds!! Materials scientists will fawn over the former, while chemists will discover the latter. In the end, this 'future-element' might not even react in any useful ways, but may act as a perfect catalyst for fusion, allowing us to finally realise controlled fusion reactions! Or it may act as a catalyst for producing large amounts of bose-einstein condensates...  There are so many possibilities when discussing a 'new element,' that we don't need to limit the discussion to what its properties might be. It's also important to look at the properties of things it mixes/ reacts with, or the benefits it confers onto other reactions.

The stability of larger elements comes from the acceleration of the gravity well they reside in.   Without the immense accelerative pressure, they wouldn't hold together.  That's how you eventually get to a neutron star.  The remaining matter is just neutrons packed together simply because of immense gravity.  Gravity wells are the way that matter, starting as hydrogen at the big bang, gets transmuted through progressively deeper gravity wells into denser and denser forms of matter.  Gravity is the recycler of matter back into the pure potential you'd find within a blackhole

Understanding element 43's instability is signifiant to understanding atomic properties.  It is very likely that atomic mass including neutrons plays a significant role in the stability and half life of an element.  It is likely an instability that the ratio of the mass of an element versus the force that holds atoms together will have either electrons or protons decay to a stable slow moving element.  It is very likely that manipulating electromagnetic forces or creating a stronger electromagnetic field or diamagnetic field will alter the speed of decay or create a new way to stabilize elements.  It is like how the moon right now is in orbit around the Earth.  Slowly that orbit is decaying to the point that it will no longer orbit the sun or shift its orbit away. Many of those science fiction "materials" have unique physical or chemical properties unseen otherwise.  Which is really largely in the realm of the electron configurations, not so much nuclear properties.  Only some are impacted by the configuration of the nucleus (the fuels and Kryptonite.)  So I'm afraid that even if we discover a lot of new elements, those fantasy physical properties still wouldn't exist.  Those new "super materials," not necessarily elements, could be made by using the current elements instead.  Look at plain old Carbon, one of the first elements discovered.  Now put it in a matrix not seen in nature; ta-da, Carbon fiber, with amazing properties.  Nothing quite like it in nature, but nothing new was needed, either.

As for the anthropic discussion at the end of this video my thinking is as follows.  We can reasonably assume... and we do... that the laws of physics are the same everywhere in the universe.  We have learned about these laws.... but of course the why's.... why do these laws exist at the critical .... seemingly fine tuned ...... values they do to give rise to beings like ourselves... this remains a mystery.  Nevertheless these laws of physics do exist and were equally distributed throughout the cosmos with the big bang.  Similar reasoning applies to the laws of chemistry.  Think of it.  For some period of time.... I think some billions of years... after the creation of the universe.... there were no chemical reactions... only nuclear.... for until the first stars formed and died and exploded seeding the universe with heavier elements.... in the beginning all that existed was hydrogen and helium.  There is no chemistry to be done  at this point.  But what of what would later on become the laws of chemistry.  Did they only arise at the point that heavier elements were created to begin chemical reactions..... or did these laws already exists at the start with the big bang.... but could not yet manifest until the elements needed for such reactions were created by exploding stars.  It seems reasonable that the laws of chemistry that would ultimately arise were built into the universe from the very beginning.  Well the same then is likely true with the laws of biochemistry.... though for instance we do not understand why life came to be...we can very readily observe how the molecules of life work down at the cellular level... with DNA and proteins and all.  Why these laws of biochemistry exist...as with physics..... we do not understand... be we do understand much of how it works.  So it seems reasonable then that with the big bang the laws of physics and chemistry and biology were equally distributed throughout the universe from the very start just awaiting the natural physical formation of the needed materials to manifest.  If so then it's reasonable  to conclude that life exists throughout the universe.  But look at the history of life on earth.  Indeed.... following my reasoning... the laws of biochemistry might very well dictate that it takes billions of years for evolution to create intelligent life.  99% of the time of earth life was very primitive single cell stuff.  Our advanced human civilization with technology and our ability to even imagine alien space travel is a micro second in time compared to the billions of years of the universe.  Coupled with the vastness of the universe and the trillions upon trillions of planets to visit and the pure luck of visiting within the mere centuries of time of advanced life ...... I do not find it mysterious at all that we have yet to be visited or detect other alien life.  But as my arguement goes... the laws that ultimately gave rise to our life form and our abilities likely exist throughout the universe.  Life and advanced life is out there and even if we have yet to have an alien encounter it may very well be that other worlds have discovered each other.  I will take it one step further... pure speculation yes... but reasonably one could argue that the laws of life are simply DNA... that remarkable and unexplainable as the DNA molecule is and how it controls protein structure... that its simply the way it is... like physics and chemistry. .. the laws of biology... though seeminly magical and unexplainable that it could even arise...... nevertheless exist everywhere.  As with laws of physics and chemistry we do not understand how or why it came to be and yet there it is.  There is already evidence of such as space rocks are shown to have the building block materials of DNA.  So perhaps star trek and star wars.... with so many humanoid types of beings... maybe its not so far fetched.  Yes there is evolution and random selection... but other worlds would be subject to similar stresses and organisms would pursue survival according to the laws of evolution.    It could be... again why we do not know... but it could be built into the laws that not only does DNA give rise to life but things like back bones... reproduction methods starting with self replication to eggs to advancing to live birth... milk... caring for young... etc... these are simply pre-built laws spread everywhere throughout the universe.... so not only is life everywhere but perhaps even the structure of life forms itself. .. it was all built in from the start.  Not sure about this humanoid idea... or say mammal structure idea... but I do believe life itself in any form exists throughout the universe and DNA and all the creatures this remarkable material gives rise to is the chemistry set of the cosmos and built in from the start.

When it comes to finding stable heavy isotopes, Astrophysicists are way ahead of nuclear physicists. Astrophysicists have already detected perfectly stable isotopes beyond the island of stability, with mass-numbers in the order of 10^57. We usually refer to them as neutron stars.  (And yes, though they are called neutron stars, they still have some protons inside. Though they'll make less than 1% of the neutron stars total mass)

Interesting enough technitium 99m is used for hear scans because it sticks to damaged heart muscle. After it decays by an internal transition by a gamma emission you are left with technitium 99 which is a rather weak beta emitter. One curious property of Tc99 is that it is an amazingly potent corrosion inhibitor for stainless steel despite its radioavtibity. In fact the radioactivity itself is what makes it so potent. Each beta reduces multiple oxides and hydroxides back to metals. It is added to reactor steels used in submarines for this reason because the added radioactivity is negligable and the reactor is cooled with salt water. ğŸ¤“

Element zero does exist, it is formed of free netrons, and usually called mononeutron matter by particle physicists. It has also been called neutronium (symbol Nu or -).  There are also at least two exotic elements, positronium (Ps) and muonium (Nu), they form compounds similarly to hydrogen, but are a lot lighter. They and their compounds have been given names by the IUPAC, but sadly they have not been recognized as elements. Another exotic element that may exist is tauonium.

My comments are mostly on Matt's comment responses. I've probably mentioned this before, but my own "head canon" solution to the Fermi Paradox is that, grabby or not, it's just too expensive to venture much beyond your own solar system. Heck we made it to our own moon but after a few trips it's been another 50 years before we've made any effort to go back again. Asteroids may well be rich enough in elements rare on Earth to be profitable to mine, but have we really made any effort to do so? We've thought of more than one way to potentially reach other stars but the distances are so vast that implementing any of them is too expensive to contemplate until we get a better idea of exactly what's out there, so we wait for exoplanet searches to get detailed enough to be able to tell us more than the scant details we have now. In short, I don't think we'll find the Great Filter in the realm of biology or physics, but in economics.

Yes and no.  Yes, because we can keep smashing protons together to forge new elements.  No, because the mythical "region of stability" does not exist, and no element will ever be stable beyond a certain point.  It's like dark matter, or aether.  It doesn't exist.
The problem with elements in this "island of stability" is that this is relative to its neighbors. They'd still likely be fiercely radioactive. Even if they were completely stable and useful in some way it would be tremendously expensive to make so in the end would be nothing more than fodder for a few research papers and a Nobel prize in chemistry.

Why are there such vast differences in properties between adjacent elements on the periodic table? At the pressures and temperatures on Earth's surface, Helium is an inert gas and Lithium is a solid grey metal, even though they are only one proton apart. Mercury is a liquid at terrestrial temperatures and pressure, yet surrounded by more traditional metals. Are adjacent elements more similar in the vacuum and temperature of space? Do any other properties (other than the number of protons) scale linearly as you move up the periodic table?

17:03 the issue is probability. Probability emerges from geometry. I.e. you actually need to have another example of life, at least, to do meaningful probability. But worse still, probability is just that. Why would you be surprised to be the exception? You should only be surprised if geometric evidence leads you to think you are NOT the first. And here, our data is skewed by the fact we have no other tree of life discoveries, and that we just simply do not have enough evidence.  So are we the first with good evidence or are we typical but have terrible evidence? How to know but to actually discover?  In other words, and this seems to be a point that goes over everyone's head, the probability of the existence of other beings can only be informed by... the discovery of other beings. And not the context of what it takes to birth life. That's a useful probability that will help us search for life, but it is completely disconnected to the existence of life itself. As we don't actually have a direct way of checking the probability that a "habitable zone" will birth complex life.

I found the recent BobbyBroccoli video that deep dives into the history of finding artificial elements absolutely fascinating
It would seem that the vast chasms of space that separate us from just our nearest neighbors in the Milky Way galaxy preclude us from interacting or even knowing about other life forms. And that assumes that intelligent life even exists in other star systems. Planet earth has so many unique factors that are conducive to life, and we donâ€™t see them anywhere else in our own solar system:                                       Just the right mass - just the right distance from our sun - just the right steady burning Sun - one moon just the right size and distance from our planet - an enormous neighbor planet that provides a gravity deflection for large objects in the Oort cloud that are perturbed out of their orbits - just the right angle of axis to the suns ecliptic plane - water, water, water as ice, liquid, and vapor.  Given the number of stars and galaxies we can observe there may be other planets that share these characteristics. But that doesnâ€™t guarantee intelligent beings have or will ever evolve there. For the vast majority of earthâ€™s biological history there were only giant stupid reptiles and birds who made a meal or snack of any evolving primates - the Chicxulub asteroid that hit 66 million years ago wiped out that class of predators. Finally our ancestors could roam the earth and evolve free of immanent death every time one went outside. If another similar sized asteroid hit tomorrow it could end our existence as well. So timing and luck amidst a host of independent phenomena had to be in place for us to evolve. How often do these factors come together in the universe? Probably not too often, perhaps never. And we are still quite a ways from being able to confirm that on our own. And while we remain a prickly ethnocentric species easily swayed by superstition and myth, moving toward an enlightened future is going to be pretty slow.
At one time, before I earned my degrees in biochemistry, I was tantalized by the idea of the island of stability. Now, I very much doubt that super-heavy atoms will have any properties that are unique to them that are not found in their periodic counterparts among the known elements. Besides a high likelihood of radioactivity that is. Unless we find strange quarks or other fundamental particles play a much larger role in the formation of atomic nuclei, that then has a downstream affect on electron clouds and orbitals. That is doubtful. Their uses would probably be based completely on having a greater mass while maintaining a similar chemistry to that of lighter metals in the same group. It is unlikely that they will display any metalloid or non-metal characteristics, making them even less interesting. While I certainly support trying to find such elements, purely for the greater scientific understanding it may provide, I very much doubt they will have any meaningful impact on material science or have any genuine uses that would justify the costs of production.

Have you heard of Przybylski's Star? Its a chemically peculiar star who's spectrum seems to have short lived actinides such as Californium and Einsteinium at some level of abundance. The only reasonable source of these unstable isotopes is an undiscovered, slowly decaying superheavy element concentrated in the star. I think the Copernican Principle is generally overstated. It shouldn't be that we _cannot_ be in a special place in the universe; it's that we _must not assume_ we are, but rather begin from the assumption that we are not. Since that's the most likely state of affairs, we must assign is a very high prior probability.  But to adhere to the Copernican Principle regardless of how much evidence stacks up against it in this or that domain, is to be as rigidly dogmatic as the church fathers Copernicus was bold enough to contradict.

Slight correction at 3:35. You mention that Technetium and all the heavier elements are produced in the core of giant stars and then they find their way into the rest of the cosmos via supernovae. However, it is only elements lighter and up to iron that can be forged via nuclear fusion in a star's core. After iron, there is less energy given off in fusion and therefore the star cannot sustain itself and the core collapses causing a supernova. It is during the supernova explosion that all elements heavier than iron are created.  Edit: So I forgot to add that some heavier elements can still be made via slow neutron capture and proton capture in a stars plasma, but as far as fusion goes it is only up to iron that is fused in a star's core.

Two common properties that most fictional elements have are being lightweight and extremely durable (mostly metals.) These are properties that we shouldn't expect from elements in the "island of stability", particularly the former. Quite the opposite; they'll be the heaviest materials above degenerate matter and neutron-star matter. They may in fact be used for their extreme density because of that.  Perhaps instead of creating new elements to serve the purposes of making indestructible armor and weapons for scifi-fantasy stories, composite materials made of common lighter elements like carbon and silicon should be used. Carbon nano-fibers for example may be able to have all the properties of the most amazing materials in science fiction and much more.

Regarding the "Dark Forest" solution to the Fermi Paradox that you mentioned at the end: That is one of the most easily dismissed hypotheses in the set, because the universe is NOT a dark forest. First of all, it's always daytime in space; secondly, there's so little tactical cover in space that we have direct line-of-sight to locations literally billions of light-years distant. The universe has more in common with a savannah at noon after a brush fire. Nothing can hide in space.

The number 137 is important -- called the Fine Structure Constant, if I'm not mistaken -- and Feynman was said to be obsessed with it. There's a theory that there can be no element with an atomic number greater than 137, which is probably true, though there are theorists who dispute that. If there IS a possibility for elements with more than 137 protons in their nucleus, I recommend that element 144 be called 'Grossium', since 12 squared is a 'gross', and as far as I know the two-letter element symbol 'Gr' hasn't been taken yet, so I suggest #144 be Grossium [Gr], and I've calculated that its most stable isotope -- based on the proportions of Protons-to-Neutrons of the most stable isotopes of the elements above its position in its column -- is probably one with 256 neutrons, so a total of 400 nucleons. Maybe in a hundred years we'll be hearing about Grossium-400 being synthesized in particle accelerators, who knows?

Why are some  elements stable and some unstable. What is holding them together and what is making them fall apart. Are they  magnetic or something else.
Americium smoke detectors are inferior in most respects to optical smoke detectors. They do have advantages in certain limited situations, but it's not like the world would go to flames if we ran out of americium. It's not really very important as elements go. Similarly, while plutonium reactors and technetium nuclear medicine are assets to humanity, they compete with uranium and thorium reactors and with other contrast agents for radioimaging. So they aren't really game-changers. And those are readily produced in bulk quantities from highly abundant uranium and thorium. Comparing the impact of a new transuranic element on society to the impact of the invention of bronze and iron tools is silly. We're not going to enter the unbihexium age.  If we do discover an island of stability, I think it is very unlikely the elements there will have practical applications, and even if they do, those applications are likely to be very limited. After all, they will probably be much less stable and much, much, *much* more expensive than curium, which already has hardly any practical applications. Instead, I think the significance of finding the island would be basic research. It is currently unknown if the most stable nuclide in this island will have a half life of one minute or one million years. Regarding Eliyah Zayin's criticism of the Copernican principle's use - Copernican principle is only useful for filling in the gaps of what you know with a probabilistic statement. It's not a firm claim that we are the most probable state, it's that of the remaining states we're most likely to be the most probable of them. This makes intuitive sense in simple terms - standing in some aisle of a grocery store when you hear the "we're closing in 5 minutes" message, you can predict that you'll probably end up somewhere in the middle of the line of all the remaining customers when you go to the checkout lane because there are more possible spaces in the middle than on the front or back of the group. If you roll 2 normal 6-sided dice you can predict that you'll probably get an even number because more of the possible outcomes are even. It only gets confusing when you take away more of the known aspects.  In this case, we THINK we know the habitable age range of the universe, which puts us as early but without knowing that, Copernican principle suggests we should be towards the middle. That leaves several unknown probabilities - the limited probability that we're just in a less common outcome in a the habitable age range that we're right about, the probability that we're wrong about the habitable age range and towards the more common part of it, the probability that we're right about the habitable age range but there are other factors that make this part of the range more likely than later, etc.   To go back to the dice example - if press a button that you're told rolls an unknown number of dice and you get mostly even numbers, all under 12 and all over 2, you can guess that it has 2 6-sided dice inside...until you roll an 87 and then begin to wonder if it's a much larger set of dice and you've just had low probability outcomes or if it's 2 6-sided dice just with 1 side with a strangely large side, or if you were just not told that periodically it will change what number of dice it has, or if you were lied to and it does something other than roll dice. Copernican principle would give you a better chance of working out which scenario you're more likely in but it's not definitive, and it's not meant to be.

If a person were to look at how electrons fill up the shells in atoms: 2, 8, 18, 32, 32, 18, 8 (seven shells), and realizing that energy could freely flow in this universe if nothing stopped it from doing so, then a natural bell shaped curve might occur.  An eighth energy shell might exist with a maximum of two elements in it, chemical element #119 (8s1) and chemical element #120 (8s2).  Chemical Element #119 (8s1): #119 I put at the bottom of the Hydrogen group on the Periodic Table of the Elements.  It only has one electron in it's outer shell with room for only one more electron.  Energy might even enter the atom through the missing electron spot and then at least some of the energy might get trapped inside of the atom under the atom's outer shell.  Chemical Element #120 (8s2): #120 I put at the bottom of the Helium group since it's outer shell is full of electrons.  It might have some of the properties of group two, Beryllium group (Alkali Earth Metals group) since it has two electrons in it's outer shell; as well as some of the properties of the Helium group (Noble Gases group) since it's outer shell is full of electrons; and if you look at the step down deflection of the semi-metals and where #120 would be located on the chart, it's possible #120 might even have some semi-metal characteristics.  #120 would be the heaviest element in this universe. The proton and electron would be ejected outside of the atom since all their respective areas are full.  One proton and one electron are basic hydrogen, of which the Sun is primarily made up of, and the Sun certainly gives off neutrinos and energy.  And note, it's the neutron that split, not a proton.  So even after the split, there are still 120 protons inside of the atom and the atom still exists as element #120.  The star would last longer that way.  In addition, if the neutron that split triggered a chain reaction inside of the star, this could possibly be how stars nova, (even if only periodically).  If stars were looked at as if this theoretical idea were true, and found to even be somewhat true, then we might just have a better model of the universe to work with, even if it's not totally 100% true. Another issue with the copernican/anthropic principle: we have a lot of properties. Assuming each of those properties is normally distributed, it is reasonable to assume we would find ourselves in a "normal" range for each of those properties. HOWEVER, it is unreasonable to assume we would find ourselves in a normal range for ALL of those properties. If you look at 100 different attributes, each of which is normally distributed, one would expect that our placement within each distribution should itself be normally distributed. It would be highly anomalous if we were perfectly centered in every conceivable property.

Two things. First is idea of Islands of Stability; basic idea is perhaps a group of elements undiscovered yet will not be radioactive. Another is theory of what limits the maximum protons and electrons that can be in an atom; the speed of light in this theory is part of it. Basically while electrons do not move in atomic orbitals in normal sense, there is still some vector and velocity as part of their behavior. So...the higher the orbital the closer electrons get to light speed, and light speed is unreachable. Also electrons repel electrons so inner orbital electrons would counteract or neutralize some percentage of attractive force towards protons...the more orbitals the more this would happen. But both are admittedly logical while also probably only at best aproximations of the real physics. With ? other phenomena possible. I respectfully disagree with the idea, superheavy elements in the predicted isle of stability could exert half-lifes in the region of millions of years. We don't have the ingredients to make what are almost surely the more stable isotopes of the existing high atomic number elements.  We just don't have anything neutron rich enough to start with, and if you look at a table of nuclides (the one that the Korean Atomic Energy Research Institute hosts is my favorite online version) the result is clear.  Every known isotope of all of the elements above 110 or 112 are quite neutron deficient.    I'm not saying that I think some isotope of element 116 (just to pick one) would be stable if we could make any isotope of that element, but if we can make something in the 120s, it's got no chance to be any of the more stable ones, let alone the most.

No, because an element is defined by its number of protons.  It's really that simple.  Unknown elements no, unknown compounds are likely to exist.  Don't confuse elements with compounds. An isotope with an odd number of protons and an odd number of neutrons does not have the spins of the extra protons and neutrons cancel out since their shells are filled separately. In most cases they'll decay by beta decay to an adjacent element with an even number of protons (and the daughter isotope will have an even number of neutrons since the mother isotope had an even number of nucleons). There are a few exceptions: very light isotopes (H-2, Li-6.B-10, N-14, where decay would lead to a serious imbalance between protons and neutrons) and possibly Tantalum-180m, which has a spin of 9 and nothing it can decay into with a spin greater than 1. Some others (K-40, V-50, Lanthanum-138, Lutetium-176) also have high spins but are radioactive with very long half-lives.  Or to simply, odd-odd is less stable than even-even except for very light elements and one weirdo.

The spin-coupling magic numbers for nucleons are just like the Chicken McNugget numbers:  McNuggets are only sold in boxes of 6, 9, and 20 (excluding Happy Meals). Therefore, for a McDonalds order to contain a given number of McNuggets, that number must be the sum of any multiple of the numbers 6, 9, and 20. If the order contains a request for a number of McNuggets that cannot be so described, that number is unstable. Er, impossible.  Most interestingly of all, the highest number of McNuggets that is unorderable is... 43! The Technetium of fast food orders! This surely cannot be a coincidence.  The number 43 being unorderable both as an aliquot of McNuggets _and_ as an element, a mere coincidence? Pah! Next you'll be telling me that both inertial _and_ gravitational mass having precisely the same magnitude is also purely coincidental. Nope. Clearly this points to a fundamental property of the universe, as yet not understood.  Interestingly, however, any number of McNuggets above 43 is indeed stable, which suggests they may prove superior to chemical elements as a way to obtain materials with exotic and useful properties. We should divert significant funding to study of this area.

5:08 fun fact: Due to the fact that C14 only occurs in the atmosphere and is therefore photosynthesized by plants, all plant matter contains a defined amount of C14 atoms, which is constant up to the point of their death. However: fossil fuels do not contain C14. This fact can be used to determine if a product is based on renewables or a fossil fuel based feed stock and also whats the ratio between both feed stocks is.

I think a reasonable question would be if there's an island of stability then why don't we see stable supermassive elements in nature.  To hypothesize on that question I would suggest a couple different theories: either they are so massive that they only precipitate out of accretion disks in the centers and so we'd only observe these elements on inner planets and as contaminants in stars. Or another hypothesis for why we wouldn't see them could be that there's not enough energy expenditure in nature to make these elements occur regularly, which means we'd probably need to go to a very energetic place like a galactic arm or something like that in order to observe these materials and even there they would be rare.

The only thing which would dissuade me from believing in semi stable plateaus on the periodic table at very high proton+neutron content is how much weaker the strong force would be over the size of those atoms nucleus.  What i believe to be the case for the very heavy atoms is that the repulsive electromagnetic force overcomes the strong force over the size of the nucleus of the atom, if that is the case there can be no semi stable plateau above what we have already found.
